class="css-1vkm6nb ehdk2mb0"><h1 class="css-1s4ffep e1h9rw200" id="link-ffe62c" itemprop="headline"><span>Whistle-Blower Complaint Sets Off a Battle Involving Trump </span></h1></div><p class="css-1ifw933 e1wiw3jv0">The complaint, from a member of the intelligence community, remained opaque but involved at least one of the president’s communications with a foreign leader. </p><figure aria-label="media" class="sizeMedium css-1ca6zxf" itemprop="associatedMedia" itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/VideoObject" role="group"><meta content="2019-09-19T18:00:35.000Z" class="css-6n7j50"><button aria-expanded="false" aria-haspopup="true" aria-label="More sharing options" class="css-16ogagc" data-testid=""><svg class="css-uhuo44" height="13" viewbox="0 0 16 13" width="16"><path clip-rule="evenodd" d="M15.406 5.359L8.978 0v3.215C3.82 3.215.406 8.107.406 12.66 1.653 9.133 4.29 7.517 8.978 7.517v3.2l6.428-5.358z" fill="#000" fill-rule="evenodd"></path></svg></button></div></li><li class="css-60hakz"></li><li class="css-l72opv"></li></ul></div></div></li></ul></div></header></div><section class="meteredContent css-1i2y565" itemprop="articleBody" name="articleBody"><div class="css-1fanzo5 StoryBodyCompanionColumn"><div class="css-53u6y8"><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">WASHINGTON — A potentially explosive complaint by a whistle-blower in the intelligence community said to involve President Trump emerged on Thursday as the latest front in a continuing oversight dispute between administration officials and House Democrats.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">The complaint touched off speculation about its allegations, which remained shrouded in mystery. It involves at least one instance of Mr. Trump making an unspecified commitment to a foreign leader and includes other actions, according to interviews. At least part of the allegation deals with Ukraine, two people familiar with it said. </p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">The complaint, submitted by a member of the intelligence community to its inspector general, renewed questions about how the president handles delicate information. Mr. Trump defended his actions, and allies described his style with foreign leaders as more freewheeling than typical high-level diplomacy. “I would only do what is right anyway, and only do good for the USA!” Mr. Trump <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://twitter.com/realDonaldTrump/status/1174696521310363649" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">wrote on Twitter</a>.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">The controversy first erupted a week ago, when Representative Adam B. Schiff, Democrat of California and the chairman of the House Intelligence Committee, revealed the existence of the complaint and disclosed that the acting director of national intelligence, Joseph Maguire, <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2019/09/17/us/politics/dni-whistleblower-complaint.html?module=inline" title="">had blocked</a> the inspector general from sharing it with Congress, as generally required by law. The inspector general deemed the complaint legitimate and opened an inquiry.</p></div><aside class="css-o6xoe7"></aside></div><div class="css-1fanzo5 StoryBodyCompanionColumn"><div class="css-53u6y8"><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">Mr. Maguire’s intervention touched off the latest in <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2019/05/07/us/politics/trump-democrats.html?module=inline" title="">a series of clashes</a> between congressional Democrats seeking to conduct oversight and administration officials who they say are stonewalling their requests for information. Democrats accused Mr. Maguire of ignoring the law, possibly to protect Mr. Trump or another high-level official, though intelligence officials insisted that they blocked lawmakers’ access to the complaint in accordance with the law, not politics.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">A spokeswoman for Mr. Maguire declined to comment. <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://compassrosepllc.com/bakaj/" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">Andrew P. Bakaj</a>, a former C.I.A. and Pentagon official whose legal practice specializes in whistle-blower and security clearance issues, confirmed that he was representing the official who filed the complaint. Mr. Bakaj declined to identify his client and said he would not disclose details of the complaint.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">For nearly a week, the controversy remained opaque. </p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">Then on Wednesday evening, <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.washingtonpost.com/national-security/trumps-communications-with-foreign-leader-are-part-of-whistleblower-complaint-that-spurred-standoff-between-spy-chief-and-congress-former-officials-say/2019/09/18/df651aa2-da60-11e9-bfb1-849887369476_story.html" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">The Washington Post reported</a> that the whistle-blower’s allegations centered on at least one conversation involving Mr. Trump, setting off another frenzy in Trump-era Washington. The inspector general, Michael Atkinson, appeared on Capitol Hill in a closed-door session on Thursday but divulged no specifics beyond <!-- -->saying<!-- --> that the complaint involved multiple actions, according to two officials familiar with his briefing. </p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">He would not say whether the complaint involved the president, according to committee members. But separately, a person familiar with the whistle-blower’s complaint said it involved in part a commitment that Mr. Trump made in a communication with another world leader. No single communication was at the root of the complaint, another person familiar with it said. </p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">The intelligence official filed the formal whistle-blower complaint on Aug. 12. <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.dni.gov/ICIG-Whistleblower/" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">Such a complaint</a> is lodged through a formal process intended to protect a whistle-blower from retaliation.</p></div><aside class="css-o6xoe7"></aside></div><div class="css-1fanzo5 StoryBodyCompanionColumn"><div class="css-53u6y8"><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">Though it is not clear how exactly Ukraine fits into the allegations, questions have already emerged about Mr. Trump’s dealing with its government. He spoke on July 25 with President Volodymyr Zelensky and said he was convinced that Ukraine’s new government would quickly improve the country’s image and investigate corruption, which “inhibited the interaction” between the two nations, according to a Ukrainian government <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.president.gov.ua/en/news/volodimir-zelenskij-proviv-telefonnu-rozmovu-z-prezidentom-s-56617" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">summary of the call</a>.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">Mr. Trump’s close allies, including his personal lawyer Rudolph W. Giuliani, were also urging the Ukrainian government to investigate matters that could help Mr. Trump by embarrassing his political rivals, including former Vice President Joseph R. Biden Jr.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">According to government officials who handle foreign policy in the United States and Ukraine, Mr. Giuliani’s efforts created the impression that the Trump administration’s willingness to back Mr. Zelensky was linked to his government’s readiness to in turn pursue the investigations sought by Mr. Trump’s allies.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">Mr. Giuliani said he did not know whether Mr. Trump discussed those matters with Mr. Zelensky, but argued it would not be inappropriate.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">The president has the right to tell another country’s leader to investigate corruption, particularly if it “bleeds over” into the United States, Mr. Giuliani said on Thursday. “If I were president, I would say that,” he added.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">Around the same time, a separate issue was brewing. Congressional aides and administration officials who work on Ukraine issues had become concerned that the White House was slow-walking a military assistance package for Kiev, according to people involved in an effort to free up the assistance.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">Last week, the two issues merged when Mr. Schiff and two other Democratic House committee chairmen requested the transcript of Mr. Trump’s call with Mr. Zelensky from the State Department and the White House as part of <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2019/09/09/us/politics/trump-ukraine-house-investigation.html?module=inline" title="">an investigation</a> into whether Mr. Trump and Mr. Giuliani were misappropriating the American foreign policy apparatus for political gain. </p></div><aside class="css-o6xoe7"></aside></div><div class="css-1fanzo5 StoryBodyCompanionColumn"><div class="css-53u6y8"><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">The Democrats indicated they planned to examine whether the delay in the assistance “is part of President Trump’s effort to coerce the Ukrainian government into pursuing politically motivated investigations.”</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">The next day, <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://intelligence.house.gov/uploadedfiles/20190910_-_chm_schiff_letter_to_acting_dni_maguire.pdf" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">Mr. Schiff wrote to Mr. Maguire</a> seeking information about the whistle-blower complaint. </p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">And the following day, the White House <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2019/09/12/us/politics/ukraine-aid-white-house.html?module=inline" title="">released the military assistance</a> to Ukraine, with little explanation.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">The unusual disagreement between Mr. Maguire and Mr. Atkinson centers on who is best suited to investigate the whistle-blower’s accusations. </p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">In a letter to the leaders of the House Intelligence Committee, Mr. Atkinson wrote that the complaint falls within the jurisdiction of the director of national intelligence and “relates to one of the most significant and important of the D.N.I.’s responsibilities to the American people.” </p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">Mr. Maguire has not disputed the seriousness of the allegation but determined in consultation with the Justice Department that it was outside the scope of the law requiring whistle-blower complaints be forwarded to Congress. Any accusation that triggers the requirement must involve the funding, administration or operations of an intelligence agency. </p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">Administration officials have shared at least some details of the accusations with the White House, to allow officials to weigh whether to assert executive privilege, an official said.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">Some current and former officials defended Mr. Maguire’s decision to consult with the Justice Department and the White House. Any question of whether a presidential communication was subject to executive privilege would be a White House decision, and the Justice Department is supposed to offer legal advice.</p></div><aside class="css-o6xoe7"></aside></div><div class="css-1fanzo5 StoryBodyCompanionColumn"><div class="css-53u6y8"><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">“But given the recent history of Justice Department leadership engaging in public messaging that comes across as scripted by the White House, it is not unreasonable to have concerns about the consultation with the department in this instance,” said David H. Laufman, who served as chief of the counterintelligence section in the Justice Department’s National Security Division.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">Mr. Schiff said he would explore potential recourse with the House’s general counsel to try to force the release of the complaint, including potentially suing for it in court.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">The law is “very clear” that the whistle-blower complaint must be handed over to Congress, said Senator Angus King of Maine, an independent who caucuses with the Democrats.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">“The inspector general determines what level of concern it is,” Mr. King, a member of the Senate Intelligence Committee, said in an interview. “Once the determination is made,” he added, the director of national intelligence “has a ministerial responsibility to share that with Congress — it is not discretionary.” </p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0"><em class="css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0">[Read </em><a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://int.nyt.com/data/documenthelper/1817-dni-letter-1-to-schiff/4ecb7c343981a8678ac4/optimized/full.pdf#page=1" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title=""><em class="css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0">a pair</em></a><em class="css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0"> </em><a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://int.nyt.com/data/documenthelper/1818-dni-letter-2-to-schiff/4ecb7c343981a8678ac4/optimized/full.pdf#page=1" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title=""><em class="css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0">of letters</em></a><em class="css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0"> from the Office of the Director of National Intelligence about th</em><em class="css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0">e comp</em><em class="css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0">laint.]</em> </p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">Democrats emerged from Mr. Atkinson’s briefing and renewed their accusation that the Trump administration was orchestrating a cover-up of an urgent and legitimate whistle-blower complaint that could affect national security.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">Mr. Schiff told reporters after the briefing that he still did not know the contents of the complaint and had been unable to get an answer to whether the White House was involved in suppressing it.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">“I don’t think this is a problem of the law,” he said. “I think the law is written very clearly. I think the law is just fine. The problem lies elsewhere. And we’re determined to do everything we can to determine what this urgent concern is, to make sure that the national security is protected and to make sure that this whistle-blower is protected.”</p></div><aside class="css-o6xoe7"></aside></div><div class="css-1fanzo5 StoryBodyCompanionColumn"><div class="css-53u6y8"><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">Officials<!-- --> with Mr. Maguire’s office were scrambling to find a compromise with Congress ahead of Mr. Maguire’s scheduled testimony on Sept. 26, according to a senior intelligence official.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">Senator Mark Warner of Virginia, the top Democrat on the Senate intelligence panel, said on Thursday that he and the committee’s Republican chairman, Senator Richard Burr of North Carolina, also expected both the inspector general and acting director to brief them early next week and “clear this issue up.”</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">Some<!-- --> legal experts said it was not obvious how an exchange between Mr. Trump and a foreign leader could meet the legal standards for a whistle-blower complaint that the inspector general would deem an “urgent concern.”</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">Under <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.law.cornell.edu/uscode/text/50/3033" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">the law</a>, the complaint has to concern the existence of an intelligence activity that violates the law, rules or regulations, or otherwise amounts to mismanagement, waste, abuse or a danger to public safety. But a conversation between two foreign leaders is not itself an intelligence activity.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">And while Mr. Trump may have discussed intelligence activities with the foreign leader, he enjoys broad power as president to declassify intelligence secrets, order the intelligence community to act and otherwise direct the conduct of foreign policy as he sees fit, legal experts said.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">Mr. Trump regularly speaks with foreign leaders and is often unfettered. Some current and former officials said that what an intelligence official took to be a troubling commitment could have been an innocuous comment. </p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">Mr. Trump’s calls with other leaders are unlike anything his predecessors engaged in, one European diplomat said. The president eschews the kind of structured calls of his predecessors and instead quickly moves from the stated topic of the call to others. He will disclose his ideas for forthcoming summit meetings and test ideas and policies in a seemingly casual way, the diplomat said.</p><p class="css-exrw3m evys1bk0">But the whistle-blower complaint renewed questions about whether some of his freelance proposals were inappropriate. The accusation, even with few details, quickly gained traction in part because of longstanding concerns <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2019/05/25/us/politics/trump-intelligence-agencies.html?module=inline" title="">among some intelligence officials</a> that the information they share with the president <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2019/05/24/us/politics/trump-barr-declassify-intelligence.html?module=inline" title="">is being politicized</a>. </p><p class="css-jwz2nf etfikam0">Adam Goldman, Eileen Sullivan, Charlie Savage and Kenneth P. Vogel contributed reporting.</p></div><aside class="css-o6xoe7"></aside></div></section><div class="bottom-of-article"><div class="css-1ubp8k9"></div><div class="css-wg1cha"><div class="css-19hdyf3 e1e7j8ap0"><div><p>Julian E. 13.1L57.9 17l-2.5-2v-1.2l4.7-3.2v-.1l-2.4-3.6-5.2 2.8v6.6l-1 .8.2.2.9-.7 3.4 2.5 4.5-3.6-.1-.4zm-5-1.7V8.5l.2-.1 2.2 3.5-2.4 1.5zM76.7 8l-.7.5-1.9-1.6-2.2 2 .9.9v7.5l-2.4-1.5V9.6l.8-.5-2.3-2.2-2.2 2 .9.9V17l-.3.2-2.1-1.5v-6c0-1.4-.7-1.8-1.5-2.3-.7-.5-1.1-.8-1.1-1.5 0-.6.6-.9.9-1.1v-.2c-.8 0-2.9.8-2.9 2.7 0 1 .5 1.4 1 1.9s1 .9 1 1.8v5.8l-1.1.8.2.2 1-.8 2.3 2 2.5-1.7 2.8 1.7 5.3-3.1V9.2l1.3-1-.2-.2zm18.6-5.5l-1 .9-2.2-2-3.3 2.4V1.6h-.3l.1 16.2c-.3 0-1.2-.2-1.9-.4l-.2-13.5c0-1-.7-2.4-2.5-2.4s-3 1.4-3 2.8h.3c.1-.6.4-1.1 1-1.1s1.1.4 1.1 1.7v3.9c-1.8.1-2.9 1.1-2.9 2.4 0 .8.4 2 1.6 2V13c-.4-.2-.5-.5-.5-.7 0-.6.5-.8 1.3-.8h.4v6.2c-1.5.5-2.1 1.6-2.1 2.8 0 1.7 1.3 2.9 3.3 2.9 1.4 0 2.6-.2 3.8-.5 1-.2 2.3-.5 2.9-.5.8 0 1.1.4 1.1.9 0 .7-.3 1-.7 1.1v.2c1.6-.3 2.6-1.3
